margin-right: -0.387ex; width:12.686ex; height:6.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where:\n</p>\n<ul><li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mu \\,\\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956; --></mi>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mu \\,\\!}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mu\\,\\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8a66ee9ba05f9085943a89364f6c674143a1881f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:1.789ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravitational_parameter" title="Standard gravitational parameter">standard gravitational parameter</a>,</li>\n<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle a\\,\\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle a\\,\\!}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="a\\,\\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/99a94f96d2455b9d7faf3cec3eb02ab3c455aec1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:1.617ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">hyperbola</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>The hyperbolic excess velocity is related to the <a href="/wiki/Specific_orbital_energy" title="Specific orbital energy">specific orbital energy</a> or characteristic energy by\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 2\\epsilon =C_{3}=v_{\\infty }^{2}\\,\\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>&#1013;<!-- &#1013; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>C</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8734;<!-- &#8734; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 2\\epsilon =C_{3}=v_{\\infty }^{2}\\,\\!}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="2\\epsilon=C_3=v_{\\infty}^2\\,\\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4a645f1ca055eb96daab35f0bf1a38335c2800f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:14.41ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Calculating_trajectories">Calculating trajectories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Calculating trajectories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Kepler.27s_equation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kepler\'s_equation">Kepler\'s equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Kepler\'s equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>One approach to calculating orbits (mainly used historically) is to use <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_equation" title="Kepler\'s equation">Kepler\'s equation</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle M=E-\\epsilon \\cdot \\sin E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>&#1013;<!-- &#1013; --></mi>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mi>sin</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle M=E-\\epsilon \\cdot \\sin E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt=" M = E - \\epsilon \\cdot \\sin E " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ff78f4e700fcf528ed688ac9d103d605c7fd7443" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:17.798ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>.</dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>M</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Mean_anomaly" title="Mean anomaly">mean anomaly</a>, <i>E</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Eccentric_anomaly" title="Eccentric anomaly">eccentric anomaly</a>, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\displaystyle \\epsilon }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#1013;<!-- &#1013; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\displaystyle \\epsilon }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\displaystyle \\epsilon " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8efd5695b90e542017db96e9e41b204c4f8e3cd9" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.944ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a>.\n</p><p>With Kepler\'s formula, finding the time-of-flight to reach an angle (<a href="/wiki/True_anomaly" title="True anomaly">true anomaly</a>) of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\theta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\theta }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\theta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e5ab2664b422d53eb0c7df3b87e1360d75ad9af" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.09ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">periapsis</a> is broken into two steps:\n</p>\n<ol><li>Compute the eccentric anomaly <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> from true anomaly <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\theta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\theta }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\theta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e5ab2664b422d53eb0c7df3b87e1360d75ad9af" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.09ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></li>\n<li>Compute the time-of-flight <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle t}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle t}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.84ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> from the eccentric anomaly <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></li></ol>\n<p>Finding the eccentric anomaly at a given time (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Equation#Inverse_problem" title="Kepler\'s Equation">the inverse problem</a>) is more difficult. Kepler\'s equation is <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_function" title="Transcendental function">transcendental</a> in <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, meaning it cannot be solved for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_function" title="Algebraic function">algebraically</a>. Kepler\'s equation can be solved for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Analytic_function" title="Analytic function">analytically</a> by inversion.\n</p><p>A solution of Kepler\'s equation, valid for all real values of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\textstyle \\epsilon }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d7a607cfd7220d79ee384166b8b99ad931b00439" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.944ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is:\n</p><p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E={\\begin{cases}\\displaystyle \\sum _{n=1}^{\\infty }{\\frac {M^{\\frac {n}{3}}}{n!}}\\lim _{\\theta \\to 0}\\left({\\frac {\\mathrm {d} ^{\\,n-1}}{\\mathrm {d} \\theta ^{\\,n-1}}}\\left({\\frac \\right)\n\\right)\n,  &amp; \\epsilon = 1  \\\\\n\n\\displaystyle \\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty}\n{ \\frac{ M^n }{ n! } }\n\\lim_{\\theta \\to 0} \\left(\n\\frac{\\mathrm{d}^{\\,n-1}}{\\mathrm{d}\\theta^{\\,n-1}} \\left(\n \\frac{ \\theta }{ \\theta - \\epsilon \\cdot \\sin(\\theta)} ^n \\right)\n\\right)\n, &amp;  \\epsilon \\ne  1\n\n\\end{cases}\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/431f6158a62d71404b473fa462301971485c4fdb" style="vertical-align: -6.671ex; width:57.949ex; height:14.509ex;"/></span>\n</p><p>Evaluating this yields:\n</p><p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline }}M-{\\frac {\\epsilon }{(1-\\epsilon )^{4}}}{\\frac {M^{3}}{3!}}+{\\frac {(9\\epsilon ^{2}+\\epsilon )}{(1-\\epsilon )^{7}}}{\\frac {M^{5}}{5!}}-{\\frac {(225\\epsilon ^{3}+54\\epsilon ^{2}+\\epsilon class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>9</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow>\n<mn>9</mn>\n<mo>!</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8943;<!-- &#8943; --></mo>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<mi>&#1013;<!-- &#1013; --></mi>\n<mo>&#8800;<!-- &#8800; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n<mo fence="true" stretchy="true" symmetric="true"></mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E={\\begin{cases}\\displaystyle x+{\\frac {1}{60}}x^{3}+{\\frac {1}{1400}}x^{5}+{\\frac {1}{25200}}x^{7}+{\\frac {43}{17248000}}x^{9}+{\\frac {1213}{7207200000}}x^{11}+{\\frac {151439}{12713500800000}}x^{13}\\cdots \\ |\\ x=(6M)^{\\frac {1}{3}},&amp;\\epsilon =1\\\\\\\\\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{1-\\epsilon }}M-{\\frac {\\epsilon }{(1-\\epsilon )^{4}}}{\\frac {M^{3}}{3!}}+{\\frac {(9\\epsilon ^{2}+\\epsilon )}{(1-\\epsilon )^{7}}}{\\frac {M^{5}}{5!}}-{\\frac {(225\\epsilon ^{3}+54\\epsilon ^{2}+\\epsilon )}{(1-\\epsilon )^{10}}}{\\frac {M^{7}}{7!}}+{\\frac {(11025\\epsilon ^{4}+4131\\epsilon ^{3}+243\\epsilon ^{2}+\\epsilon )}{(1-\\epsilon )^{13}}}{\\frac {M^{9}}{9!}}\\cdots ,&amp;\\epsilon \\neq 1\\end{cases}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt=" \nE =  \n\\begin{cases} \\displaystyle\nx + \\frac{1}{60} x^3 + \\frac{1}{1400}x^5 + \\frac{1}{25200}x^7 + \\frac{43}{17248000}x^9 + \\frac{ 1213}{7207200000 }x^{11} +\n \\frac{151439}{12713500800000 }x^{13} \\cdots \\ | \\ x = ( 6 M )^\\frac{1}{3}\n ,  &amp; \\epsilon = 1  \\\\\n\\\\\n\\displaystyle\n  \\frac{1}{1-\\epsilon} M \n- \\frac{\\epsilon}{( 1-\\epsilon)^4 } \\frac{M^3}{3!} \n+ \\frac{(9 \\epsilon^2 + \\epsilon)}{(1-\\epsilon)^7 } \\frac{M^5}{5!} \n- \\frac{(225 \\epsilon^3 + 54 \\epsilon^2 + \\epsilon ) }{(1-\\epsilon)^{10} } \\frac{M^7}{7!}\n+ \\frac{ (11025\\epsilon^4 + 4131 \\epsilon^3 + 243 \\epsilon^2 + \\epsilon ) }{(1-\\epsilon)^{13} } \\frac{M^9}{9!} \\cdots\n\n, &amp;  \\epsilon \\ne  1\n\n\\end{cases}\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/931164f1859aa2606f7dad89cea161b939380c6f" style="vertical-align: -6.534ex; margin-bottom: -0.304ex; width:125.879ex; height:14.843ex;"/></span>\n</p><p><br/>\nAlternatively, Kepler\'s Equation can be solved numerically.  First one must guess a value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and solve for time-of-flight; then adjust <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> as necessary to bring the computed time-of-flight closer to the desired value until the required precision is achieved.  Usually, <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_method" title="Newton\'s method">Newton\'s method</a> is used to achieve relatively fast convergence.\n</p><p>The main difficulty with this approach is that it can take prohibitively long to converge for the extreme elliptical orbits.  For near-parabolic orbits, eccentricity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\epsilon }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#1013;<!-- &#1013; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\epsilon }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\epsilon " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3837cad72483d97bcdde49c85d3b7b859fb3fd2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.944ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is nearly 1, and plugging <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle e=1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle e=1}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="e=1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9c2f5932668126c63c844dc00ca187bc58a29e5a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.344ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> into the formula for mean anomaly, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E-\\sin E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>sin</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E-\\sin E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E - \\sin E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/73cf586252cfbed20f189da20954eb469eac7b9b" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:9.634ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>, we find ourselves subtracting two nearly-equal values, and accuracy suffers.  For near-circular orbits, it is hard to find the periapsis in the first place (and truly circular orbits have no periapsis at all).  Furthermore, the equation was derived on the assumption of an elliptical orbit, and so it does not hold for parabolic or hyperbolic orbits.  These difficulties are what led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_variable_formulation" title="Universal variable formulation">universal variable formulation</a>, described below.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conic_orbits">Conic orbits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Conic orbits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For simple procedures, such as computing the <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a> for coplanar transfer ellipses, traditional approaches<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2009)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> are fairly effective. Others, such as time-of-flight are far more complicated, especially for near-circular and hyperbolic orbits.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_patched_conic_approximation">The patched conic approximation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: The patched conic approximation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Patched_conic_approximation" title="Patched conic approximation">Patched conic approximation</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hohmann_transfer_orbit" title="Hohmann transfer orbit">Hohmann transfer orbit</a> alone is a poor approximation for interplanetary trajectories because it neglects the planets\' own gravity.  Planetary gravity dominates the behaviour of the spacecraft in the vicinity of a planet and in most cases Hohmann severely overestimates delta-v, and produces highly inaccurate prescriptions for burn timings.\n</p><p>A relatively simple way to get a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orders_of_approximation" title="Orders of approximation">first-order approximation</a> of delta-v is based on the \'Patched Conic Approximation\' technique. One must choose the one dominant gravitating body in each region of space through which the trajectory will pass, and to model only that body\'s effects in that region.  For instance, on a trajectory from the Earth to Mars, one would begin by considering only the Earth\'s gravity until the trajectory reaches a distance where the Earth\'s gravity no longer dominates that of the Sun.  The spacecraft would be given <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> to send it on its way to interplanetary space.  Next, one would consider only the Sun\'s gravity until the trajectory reaches the neighbourhood of Mars.  During this stage, the transfer orbit model is appropriate.  Finally, only Mars\'s gravity is considered during the final portion of the trajectory where Mars\'s gravity dominates the spacecraft\'s behaviour.  The spacecraft would approach Mars on a hyperbolic orbit, and a final retrograde burn would slow the spacecraft enough to be captured by Mars.\n</p><p>The size of the "neighborhoods" (or <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence_(astrodynamics)" title="Sphere of influence (astrodynamics)">spheres of influence</a>) vary with radius <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r_{SOI}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mi>O</mi>\n<mi>I</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r_{SOI}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="r_{SOI}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1b418a5e5cb9820b94f4798c6bb142c6d23f539d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.423ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r_{SOI}=a_{p}\\left({\\frac {m_{p}}{m_{s}}}\\right)^{2/5}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>S</mi>\n<mi>O</mi>\n<mi>I</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mn>5</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r_{SOI}=a_{p}\\left({\\frac {m_{p}}{m_{s}}}\\right)^{2/5}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="r_{SOI} = a_p\\left(\\frac{m_p}{m_s}\\right)^{2/5}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4d084075d9fc44d49faff90e6229a0717055119b" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:19.866ex; height:6.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle a_{p}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle a_{p}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="a_{p}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d3a5f11f304b2976ca9d33d1575c148e791728e7" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.289ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semimajor_axis" title="Semimajor axis">semimajor axis</a> of the planet\'s orbit relative to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>; <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{p}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{p}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_p" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d7d93b292637f64f747f3fe0ea4bad9f2bd65637" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.1ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{s}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{s}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_{s}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/488560816fccdf62695552ac8bf0611a0d5c09b4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.044ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> are the <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">masses</a> of the planet and Sun, respectively.\n</p><p>This simplification is sufficient to compute rough estimates of fuel requirements, and rough time-of-flight estimates, but it is not generally accurate enough to guide a spacecraft to its destination.  For that, numerical methods are required.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_universal_variable_formulation">The universal variable formulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The universal variable formulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>To address computational shortcomings of traditional approaches for solving the 2-body problem, the <a href="/wiki/Universal_variable_formulation" title="Universal variable formulation">universal variable formulation</a> was developed.  It works equally well for the circular, elliptical, parabolic, and hyperbolic cases, the differential equations converging well when integrated for any orbit.  It also generalizes well to problems incorporating perturbation theory.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perturbations">Perturbations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Perturbations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The universal variable formulation works well with the variation of parameters technique, except now, instead of the six Keplerian orbital elements, we use a different set of orbital elements: namely, the satellite\'s initial position and velocity vectors <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle x_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle x_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="x_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86f21d0e31751534cd6584264ecf864a6aa792cf" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.384ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/60faad24775635f4722ccc438093dbbfe05f34ae" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.182ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> at a given epoch <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle t=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle t=0}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="t=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43469ec032d858feae5aa87029e22eaaf0109e9c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.101ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>.  In a two-body simulation, these elements are sufficient to compute the satellite\'s position and velocity at any time in the future, using the universal variable formulation.  Conversely, at any moment in the satellite\'s orbit, we can measure its position and velocity, and then use the universal variable approach to determine what its initial position and velocity <i>would have been</i> at the epoch.  In perfect two-body motion, these orbital elements would be invariant (just like the Keplerian elements would be).\n</p><p>However, perturbations cause the orbital elements to change over time.  Hence, we write the position element as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle x_{0}(t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle x_{0}(t)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="x_0(t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ca4c1f2c7719b7304f44a3cc5dd0416f701ac3a3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.033ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and the velocity element as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v_{0}(t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v_{0}(t)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v_0(t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81234e70ccf74821d08d2370e905d01dcb9561e0" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.831ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, indicating that they vary with time.  The technique to compute the effect of perturbations becomes one of finding expressions, either exact or approximate, for the functions <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle x_{0}(t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle x_{0}(t)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="x_0(t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ca4c1f2c7719b7304f44a3cc5dd0416f701ac3a3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.033ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v_{0}(t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v_{0}(t)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v_0(t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81234e70ccf74821d08d2370e905d01dcb9561e0" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.831ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>.\n</p><p>The following are some effects which make real orbits differ from the simple models based on a spherical earth.  Most of them can be handled on short timescales (perhaps less than a few thousand orbits) by perturbation theory because they are small relative to the corresponding two-body effects.\n</p>\n<ul><li>Equatorial bulges cause <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> of the node and the perigee</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Spherical_harmonics#Visualization_of_the_spherical_harmonics" title="Spherical harmonics">Tesseral harmonics</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> of the gravity field introduce additional perturbations</li>\n<li>Lunar and solar gravity perturbations alter the orbits</li>\n<li>Atmospheric drag reduces the semi-major axis unless make-up thrust is used</li></ul>\n<p>Over very long timescales (perhaps millions of orbits), even small perturbations can dominate, and the behaviour can become <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaotic</a>. On the other hand, the various perturbations can be orchestrated by clever astrodynamicists to assist with orbit maintenance tasks, such as <a href="/wiki/Orbital_station-keeping" title="Orbital station-keeping">station-keeping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ground_track" title="Ground track">ground track</a> maintenance or adjustment, or phasing of perigee to cover selected targets at low altitude.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_maneuver">Orbital maneuver</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Orbital maneuver">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbital_maneuver" title="Orbital maneuver">Orbital maneuver</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a>, an <b>orbital maneuver</b> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">propulsion</a> systems to change the <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>. For spacecraft far from Earth&#8212;for example those in orbits around the Sun&#8212;an orbital maneuver is called a <i>deep-space maneuver (DSM)</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources in the body of the article. (September 2011)">not verified in body</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_transfer">Orbital transfer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Orbital transfer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Transfer orbits are usually elliptical orbits that allow spacecraft to move from one (usually substantially circular) orbit to another.  Usually they require a burn at the start, a burn at the end, and sometimes one or more burns in the middle.\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Hohmann_transfer_orbit" title="Hohmann transfer orbit">Hohmann transfer orbit</a> requires a minimal <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a>.</li>\n<li>A <a href="/wiki/Bi-elliptic_transfer" title="Bi-elliptic transfer">bi-elliptic transfer</a> can require less energy than the Hohmann transfer, if the ratio of orbits is 11.94 or greater,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> but comes at the cost of increased trip time over the Hohmann transfer.</li>\n<li>Faster transfers may use any orbit that intersects both the original and destination orbits, at the cost of higher delta-v.</li>\n<li>Using low thrust engines (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_propulsion" title="Electrical propulsion">electrical propulsion</a>), if the initial orbit is supersynchronous to the final desired circular orbit then the optimal transfer orbit is achieved by thrusting continuously in the direction of the velocity at apogee. This method however takes much longer due to the low thrust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>For the case of orbital transfer between non-coplanar orbits, the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination_change" title="Orbital inclination change">change-of-plane thrust</a> must be made at the point where the orbital planes intersect (the "node"). As the objective is to change the direction of the velocity vector by an angle equal to the angle between the planes, almost all of this thrust should be made when the spacecraft is at the node near the apoapse, when the magnitude of the velocity vector is at its lowest. However, a small fraction of the orbital inclination change can be made at the node near the periapse, by slightly angling the transfer orbit injection thrust in the direction of the desired inclination change. This works because the cosine of a small angle is very nearly one, resulting in the small plane change being effectively "free" despite the high velocity of the spacecraft near periapse, as the Oberth Effect due to the increased, slightly angled thrust exceeds the cost of the thrust in the orbit-normal axis.\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Optimal_Transfer_Orbit_using_Electric_Propulsion.png/384px-Optimal_Transfer_Orbit_using_Electric_Propulsion.png 2x" width="192"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">An optimal sequence for transferring a satellite from a supersynchronous to a geosynchronous orbit using electric propulsion</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravity_assist_and_the_Oberth_effect">Gravity assist and the Oberth effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Gravity assist and the Oberth effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a>, a spacecraft swings by a planet and leaves in a different direction, at a different speed.  This is useful to speed or slow a spacecraft instead of carrying more fuel.\n</p><p>This maneuver can be approximated by an <a href="/wiki/Elastic_collision" title="Elastic collision">elastic collision</a> at large distances, though the flyby does not involve any physical contact.  Due to Newton\'s Third Law (equal and opposite reaction), any momentum gained by a spacecraft must be lost by the planet, or vice versa.  However, because the planet is much, much more massive than the spacecraft, the effect on the planet\'s orbit is negligible.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Oberth_effect" title="Oberth effect">Oberth effect</a> can be employed, particularly during a gravity assist operation. This effect is that use of a propulsion system works better at high speeds, and hence course changes are best done when close to a gravitating body; this can multiply the effective <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interplanetary_Transport_Network_and_fuzzy_orbits">Interplanetary Transport Network and fuzzy orbits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Interplanetary Transport Network and fuzzy orbits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_Transport_Network" title="Interplanetary Transport Network">Interplanetary Transport Network</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_energy_transfers" title="Low energy transfers">Low energy transfers</a></div>\n<p>It is now possible to use computers to search for routes using the nonlinearities in the gravity of the planets and moons of the Solar System. For example, it is possible to plot an orbit from high earth orbit to Mars, passing close to one of the Earth\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_point" title="Trojan point">Trojan points</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Collectively referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_Transport_Network" title="Interplanetary Transport Network">Interplanetary Transport Network</a>, these highly perturbative, even chaotic, orbital trajectories in principle need no fuel beyond that needed to reach the Lagrange point (in practice keeping to the trajectory requires some course corrections). The biggest problem with them is they can be exceedingly slow, taking many years. In addition launch windows can be very far apart.\n</p><p>They have, however, been employed on projects such as <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis</a>. This spacecraft visited the Earth-Sun <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L1" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>1</sub></a> point and returned using very little propellant.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_mechanics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation"
